      <p><b>Somalia</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup> officially the <b>Federal Republic of Somalia</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> to the Northwest, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to the east, and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to the southwest. Somalia has the longest coastline on <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>'s mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-FactbookCoastline_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FactbookCoastline-13">[12]</a></sup> Its terrain consists mainly of plateaus, plains, and highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> Hot conditions prevail year-round, with periodic monsoon winds and irregular rainfall.<sup id="cite_ref-ClimateSom_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClimateSom-14">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Somalia has an estimated population of around 15 million<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-16">[15]</a></sup> and has been described as Africa's most culturally homogeneous country.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Around 85% of its residents are ethnic <a href="/wiki/Somalis" title="Somalis">Somalis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> who have historically inhabited the country's north. Ethnic minorities are largely concentrated in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdullahi_2001_8_11_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdullahi_2001_8_11-19">[18]</a></sup> The official languages of Somalia are <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> Most people in the country are <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> the majority of them <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdullahi_2001_1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdullahi_2001_1-21">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In antiquity, Somalia was an important commercial center.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> It is among the most probable locations of the fabled ancient <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> During the Middle Ages, several powerful Somali empires dominated the regional trade, including the <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Sultanate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_the_Geledi" title="Sultanate of the Geledi">Sultanate of the Geledi</a>. </p>
      <p>In the late 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Somalia" title="Greater Somalia">Somali Sultanates</a> were colonized by <a href="/wiki/Italian_Empire" title="Italian Empire">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> European colonists merged tribal territories into two <a href="/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa" title="Colonisation of Africa">colonies</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Issa-Salwe_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Issa-Salwe-29">[28]</a></sup> but in Somaliland, <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Abdullah_Hassan" title="Mohammed Abdullah Hassan">Sayid Mohamed</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Dervish_movement_(Somali)" title="Dervish movement (Somali)">Dervish movement</a> <a href="/wiki/Somaliland_campaign" title="Somaliland campaign">managed to frustrate</a> the Abyssinians, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italians</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British</a> during expeditions against Somaliland<sup id="cite_ref-diiwaanka_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diiwaanka-30">[29]</a></sup> four times, forcing them to retreat to the coast,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> before finally being defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Somaliland_campaign_(1920)" title="Somaliland campaign (1920)">1920 Somaliland Campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Italy acquired full control of the northeastern, central, and southern parts of the area after successfully waging the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Somalia#20th_century" title="History of Somalia">Campaign of the Sultanates</a> against the ruling <a href="/wiki/Majeerteen_Sultanate" title="Majeerteen Sultanate">Majeerteen Sultanate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Hobyo" title="Sultanate of Hobyo">Sultanate of Hobyo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Issa-Salwe_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Issa-Salwe-29">[28]</a></sup> In 1960, the two territories united to form the independent <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a> under a civilian government.<sup id="cite_ref-Illustrated_Library_1967_338._33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Illustrated_Library_1967_338.-33">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Revolutionary_Council_(Somalia)" title="Supreme Revolutionary Council (Somalia)">Supreme Revolutionary Council</a> seized power in 1969 and established the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somali Democratic Republic</a>, which collapsed 22 years later, in 1991, with the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a>. During this period most regions returned to <a href="/wiki/Xeer" title="Xeer">customary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">religious law</a>. In the early 2000s, a number of interim federal administrations were created. The <a href="/wiki/Transitional_national_government,_Republic_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional national government, Republic of Somalia">Transitional National Government</a> (TNG) was established in 2000, followed by the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_federal_government,_Republic_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional federal government, Republic of Somalia">Transitional Federal Government</a> (TFG) in 2004, which reestablished the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Armed_Forces" title="Somali Armed Forces">Somali Armed Forces</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nytsnnpm_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nytsnnpm-34">[33]</a></sup> In 2006, the TFG assumed control of most of the nation's southern conflict zones from the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Islamic Courts Union">Islamic Courts Union</a> (ICU). The ICU subsequently splintered into more radical groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">Al-Shabaab</a>, which battled the TFG and its <a href="/wiki/African_Union_Mission_to_Somalia_(2007%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="African Union Mission to Somalia (2007–present)">AMISOM</a> allies for control of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By mid-2012, the insurgents had lost most of the territory they had seized, and a search for more permanent democratic institutions began.<sup id="cite_ref-Aasratdocwau_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aasratdocwau-35">[34]</a></sup> A new provisional <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Somalia" title="Constitution of Somalia">constitution</a> was passed in August 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> reforming Somalia as a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> The same month, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Government_of_Somalia" title="Federal Government of Somalia">Federal Government of Somalia</a> was formed<sup id="cite_ref-Fcacsunesinpshm_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fcacsunesinpshm-39">[38]</a></sup> and a period of reconstruction began in <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aasratdocwau_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aasratdocwau-35">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Somalia has maintained an <a href="/wiki/Informal_economy" title="Informal economy">informal economy</a> mainly based on livestock, remittances from Somalis working abroad, and telecommunications.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cbsef_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cbsef-41">[40]</a></sup> It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a> 
         <ul> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Agriculture"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Agriculture</span></a></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Somalia" title="History of Somalia">History of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg/220px-Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg/330px-Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg/440px-Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Laas_Geel_single_cow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Neolithic rock art at the <a href="/wiki/Laas_Geel" title="Laas Geel">Laas Geel</a> complex depicting a long-horned cow.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia has been inhabited since at least the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> period. During the Stone Age, the Doian and Hargeisan cultures flourished here.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> The oldest evidence of burial customs in the Horn of Africa comes from cemeteries in Somalia dating back to the 4th millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> The stone implements from the Jalelo site in the north were also characterized in 1909 as important artifacts demonstrating the archaeological universality during the Paleolithic between the East and the West.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to linguists, the first <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a>-speaking populations arrived in the region during the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period from the family's proposed <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_Urheimat" title="Afroasiatic Urheimat">urheimat</a> ("original homeland") in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Laas_Geel" title="Laas Geel">Laas Geel</a> complex on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Hargeisa" title="Hargeisa">Hargeisa</a> in northwestern Somalia dates back approximately 5,000 years, and has <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> depicting both wild animals and decorated cows.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave paintings</a> are found in the northern <a href="/wiki/Dhambalin" title="Dhambalin">Dhambalin</a> region, which feature one of the earliest known depictions of a hunter on horseback. The rock art is dated to 1,000 to 3,000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Additionally, between the towns of <a href="/wiki/Las_Khorey" title="Las Khorey">Las Khorey</a> and <a href="/wiki/El_Ayo" title="El Ayo">El Ayo</a> in northern Somalia lies <a href="/wiki/Karinhegane" title="Karinhegane">Karinhegane</a>, the site of numerous cave paintings of both real and mythical animals. Each painting has an inscription below it, which collectively have been estimated to be around 2,500 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity_and_classical_era">Antiquity and classical era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Antiquity and classical era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somali_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali Architecture">Somali Architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sesea" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesea">Sesea</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts,_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg/220px-Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg/330px-Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg/440px-Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts%2C_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1497"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Men_from_Punt_Carrying_Gifts,_Tomb_of_Rekhmire_MET_30.4.152_EGDP013029.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Men from <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a> carrying Gifts, Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Rekhmire" title="Rekhmire">Rekhmire</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramidical</a> structures, <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleums</a>, ruined cities and stone walls, such as the <a href="/wiki/Wargaade_Wall" title="Wargaade Wall">Wargaade Wall</a>, are evidence of an old civilization that once thrived in the Somali peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-Nthos_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nthos-57">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> This civilization enjoyed a trading relationship with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a> since the second millennium BCE, supporting the hypothesis that Somalia or adjacent regions were the location of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nthos_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nthos-57">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> The Puntites native to the region, traded <a href="/wiki/Myrrh" title="Myrrh">myrrh</a>, spices, gold, ebony, short-horned cattle, ivory and <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a> with the Egyptians, Phoenicians, Babylonians, Indians, Chinese and Romans through their commercial ports. An Egyptian expedition sent to Punt by the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt">18th dynasty</a> Queen <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a> is recorded on the temple reliefs at <a href="/wiki/Deir_el-Bahari" title="Deir el-Bahari">Deir el-Bahari</a>, during the reign of the Puntite King Parahu and Queen Ati.<sup id="cite_ref-Nthos_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nthos-57">[56]</a></sup> In 2015, isotopic analysis of ancient baboon mummies from Punt that had been brought to Egypt as gifts indicated that the specimens likely originated from an area encompassing eastern Somalia and the Eritrea-Ethiopia corridor.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical era</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Macrobians" title="Macrobians">Macrobians</a>, who may have been ancestral to Somalis, established a powerful tribal kingdom that ruled large parts of modern Somalia. They were reputed for their longevity and wealth, and were said to be the "tallest and handsomest of all men".<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_pg_526_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler_pg_526-61">[60]</a></sup> The Macrobians were warrior herders and seafarers. According to Herodotus' account, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Emperor">Persian Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>, upon his <a href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">conquest of Egypt</a> in 525 BC, sent ambassadors to Macrobia, bringing luxury gifts for the Macrobian king to entice his submission. The Macrobian ruler, who was elected based on his stature and beauty, replied instead with a challenge for his Persian counterpart in the form of an unstrung bow: if the Persians could manage to draw it, they would have the right to invade his country; but until then, they should thank the gods that the Macrobians never decided to invade their empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_pg_526_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler_pg_526-61">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitto2_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitto2-62">[61]</a></sup> The Macrobians were a regional power reputed for their advanced architecture and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> wealth, which was so plentiful that they shackled their prisoners in golden chains.<sup id="cite_ref-Kitto2_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitto2-62">[61]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> is believed to have been domesticated in the Horn region sometime between the 2nd and 3rd millennium BCE. From there, it spread to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the classical period, the <a href="/wiki/Sesea" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesea">Barbara</a> city-states also known as <a href="/wiki/Sesea" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesea">sesea</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mosylon" title="Mosylon">Mosylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opone" title="Opone">Opone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heis_(town)" title="Heis (town)">Mundus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulhar" title="Bulhar">Isis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malao" title="Malao">Malao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Avalites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essina" title="Essina">Essina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikon_(Somalia)" title="Nikon (Somalia)">Nikon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarapion" title="Sarapion">Sarapion</a> developed a lucrative trade network, connecting with merchants from <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Saba</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nabataean_Kingdom" title="Nabataean Kingdom">Nabataean Kingdom</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. They used the ancient Somali maritime vessel known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Beden" title="Beden">beden</a></i> to transport their cargo. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beden.jpg/220px-Beden.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beden.jpg/330px-Beden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beden.jpg/440px-Beden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Beden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Beden" title="Beden">Beden</a> is a fast, ancient Somali single or double-masted maritime ship.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Nabatea#Roman_annexation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nabatea">Roman conquest of the Nabataean Empire</a> and the Roman naval presence at <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> to curb piracy, Arab and Somali merchants agreed with the Romans to bar Indian ships from trading in the free port cities of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> to protect the interests of Somali and Arab merchants in the lucrative commerce between the Red and Mediterranean Seas.<sup id="cite_ref-EHW_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHW-65">[64]</a></sup> However, Indian merchants continued to trade in the port cities of the Somali peninsula, which was free from Roman interference.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> For centuries, Indian merchants brought large quantities of cinnamon to Somalia and Arabia from <a href="/wiki/Ceylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Spice Islands</a>. The source of the cinnamon and other spices is said to have been the best-kept secret of Arab and Somali merchants in their trade with the Roman and Greek world; the Romans and Greeks believed the source to have been the Somali peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> The collusive agreement among Somali and Arab traders inflated the price of Indian and Chinese cinnamon in North Africa, the Near East, and Europe, and made the cinnamon trade a very profitable revenue generator, especially for the Somali merchants through whose hands large quantities were shipped across sea and land routes.<sup id="cite_ref-EHW_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHW-65">[64]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_Islam_and_the_Middle_Ages">Birth of Islam and the Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Birth of Islam and the Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somali_aristocratic_and_court_titles" title="Somali aristocratic and court titles">Somali aristocratic and court titles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifat Sultanate">Ifat Sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walashma_dynasty" title="Walashma dynasty">Walashma dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mogadishu" title="Sultanate of Mogadishu">Sultanate of Mogadishu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Sultanate</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Warsangali_Sultanate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Warsangali Sultanate (page does not exist)">Warsangali Sultanate</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/290px-Silk_route.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/435px-Silk_route.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/580px-Silk_route.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2868" data-file-height="1866"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> extending from China to southern Europe, Arabia, Somalia, Egypt, Persia, India, and Java.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was introduced to the area early on by the first Muslims of Mecca fleeing prosecution during the first <a href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">Hejira</a> with <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Qiblatayn_(Somalia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Masjid al-Qiblatayn (Somalia)">Masjid al-Qiblatayn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> being built before the <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">Qiblah</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. It is one of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> In the late 9th century, <a href="/wiki/Al-Yaqubi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Yaqubi">Al-Yaqubi</a> wrote that Muslims were living along the northern Somali seaboard.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyamer_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyamer-69">[68]</a></sup> He also mentioned that the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Kingdom</a> had its capital in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyamer_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyamer-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewispohoa_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispohoa-70">[69]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Leo_Africanus" title="Leo Africanus">Leo Africanus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a> was governed by local <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a> dynasties and its realm encompassed the geographical area between the Bab el Mandeb and Cape Guardafui. It was thus flanked to the south by the <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Ajuran Empire">Ajuran Empire</a> and to the west by the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyssinian Empire">Abyssinian Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leo_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leo-71">[70]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1332, the Zeila-based King of Adal was slain in a military campaign aimed at halting Abyssinian emperor <a href="/wiki/Amda_Seyon_I" title="Amda Seyon I">Amda Seyon I</a>'s march toward the city.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> When the last Sultan of Ifat, <a href="/wiki/Sa%27ad_ad-Din_II" title="Sa'ad ad-Din II">Sa'ad ad-Din II</a>, was also killed by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Dawit_I_of_Ethiopia" class="mw-redirect" title="Dawit I of Ethiopia">Dawit I</a> in Zeila in 1410, his children escaped to Yemen, before returning in 1415.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> In the early 15th century, Adal's capital was moved further inland to the town of <a href="/wiki/Dakkar" title="Dakkar">Dakkar</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Sabr_ad-Din_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabr ad-Din II">Sabr ad-Din II</a>, the eldest son of Sa'ad ad-Din II, established a new base after his return from Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewispd_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispd-75">[74]</a></sup> <br> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg/170px-ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg/255px-ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg/340px-ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="1002"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:ShenDuGiraffePainting.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Sultanate</a> maintained commercial ties with the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and other kingdoms.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Adal's headquarters were again relocated the following century, this time southward to <a href="/wiki/Harar" title="Harar">Harar</a>. From this new capital, Adal organised an effective army led by Imam <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Ibrahim_al-Ghazi" title="Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi">Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi</a> (Ahmad "Gurey" or "Gran"; both meaning "the left-handed") that invaded the Abyssinian empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewispd_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispd-75">[74]</a></sup> This 16th-century campaign is historically known as the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian%E2%80%93Adal_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyssinian–Adal war">Conquest of Abyssinia</a> (<i>Futuh al-Habash</i>). During the war, Imam Ahmad pioneered the use of cannons supplied by the Ottoman Empire, which he imported through Zeila and deployed against Abyssinian forces and their Portuguese allies led by <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3v%C3%A3o_da_Gama" title="Cristóvão da Gama">Cristóvão da Gama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> Some scholars argue that this conflict proved, through their use on both sides, the value of firearms such as the <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a> <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">musket</a>, cannon, and the <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">arquebus</a> over traditional weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Sultanate</a> period, the sultanates and republics of <a href="/wiki/Merca" title="Merca">Merca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Barawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hobyo" title="Hobyo">Hobyo</a> and their respective ports flourished and had a lucrative foreign commerce, with ships sailing to and coming from Arabia, India, <a href="/wiki/Venetia_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Venetia (region)">Venetia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Persia, Egypt, Portugal, and as far away as China. <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>, who passed by Mogadishu in the 15th century, noted that it was a large city with houses several storeys high and large palaces in its centre, in addition to many mosques with cylindrical minarets.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harla_people" title="Harla people">Harla</a>, an early <a href="/wiki/Hamitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamitic">Hamitic</a> group of tall stature who inhabited parts of Somalia, Tchertcher and other areas in the Horn, also erected various <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">tumuli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> These masons are believed to have been ancestral to ethnic Somalis.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a> noted that many ships from the <a href="/wiki/Khambhat" title="Khambhat">Kingdom of Cambaya</a> in modern-day India sailed to Mogadishu with cloth and spices, for which they in return received gold, wax and ivory. Barbosa also highlighted the abundance of meat, wheat, barley, horses, and fruit on the coastal markets, which generated enormous wealth for the merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> Mogadishu, the center of a thriving textile industry known as <i>toob benadir</i> (specialized for the markets in Egypt, among other places<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>), together with Merca and Barawa, also served as a transit stop for <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> merchants from <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> and for the gold trade from <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> merchants from the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Hormuz</a> brought their Indian textile and fruit to the Somali coast in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> and wood.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Trading relations were established with <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a> in the 15th century,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> with cloth, ambergris and porcelain being the main commodities of the trade.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Giraffes, zebras and incense were exported to the <a href="/wiki/Ming_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming Empire">Ming Empire</a> of China, which established Somali merchants as leaders in the commerce between East Asia and the Horn.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Hindu merchants from <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a> and Southeast African merchants from <a href="/wiki/Pate_Island" title="Pate Island">Pate</a>, seeking to bypass both the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">Portuguese India</a> blockade ( and later the Omani interference), used the Somali ports of Merca and Barawa (which were out of the two powers' direct jurisdiction) to conduct their trade in safety and without interference.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_era_and_the_scramble_for_Africa">Early modern era and the scramble for Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early modern era and the scramble for Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geledi_sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Geledi sultanate">Geledi sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Majeerteen_Sultanate" title="Majeerteen Sultanate">Majeerteen Sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Hobyo" title="Sultanate of Hobyo">Sultanate of Hobyo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dervish_movement_(Somali)" title="Dervish movement (Somali)">Dervish movement (Somali)</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British Somaliland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Somaliprince.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Somaliprince.png/170px-Somaliprince.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Somaliprince.png/255px-Somaliprince.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Somaliprince.png/340px-Somaliprince.png 2x" data-file-width="626" data-file-height="668"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Somaliprince.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Gunpowder wedding of a Prince of <a href="/wiki/Luuq" title="Luuq">Luuq</a>. One of the main cities in the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Geledi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Geledi">Sultanate of Geledi</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Early modern">early modern</a> period, successor states to the Adal Sultanate and Ajuran Sultanate began to flourish in Somalia. These included the <a href="/wiki/Hiraab_Imamate" title="Hiraab Imamate">Hiraab Imamate</a>, Warsangali Sultanate, the <a href="/wiki/Bari,_Somalia" title="Bari, Somalia">Bari Dynasties</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_the_Geledi" title="Sultanate of the Geledi">Sultanate of the Geledi</a> (Gobroon dynasty), the <a href="/wiki/Majeerteen_Sultanate" title="Majeerteen Sultanate">Majeerteen Sultanate</a> (Migiurtinia), and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Hobyo" title="Sultanate of Hobyo">Sultanate of Hobyo</a> (Obbia). They continued the tradition of castle-building and seaborne trade established by previous Somali empires. </p>
      <p>Sultan <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_Mahamud_Ibrahim" title="Yusuf Mahamud Ibrahim">Yusuf Mahamud Ibrahim</a>, the third Sultan of the House of Gobroon, started the golden age of the Gobroon Dynasty. His army came out victorious during the Bardheere Jihad, which restored stability in the region and revitalized the East African <a href="/wiki/Ivory_trade" title="Ivory trade">ivory trade</a>. He also received presents from and had cordial relations with the rulers of neighbouring and distant kingdoms such as the Omani, <a href="/wiki/Wituland" title="Wituland">Witu</a> and Yemeni Sultans. </p>
      <p>Sultan Ibrahim's son <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Yusuf_(Gobroon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Yusuf (Gobroon)">Ahmed Yusuf</a> succeeded him and was one of the most important figures in 19th-century East Africa, receiving tribute from Omani governors and creating alliances with important Muslim families on the East African coast. In northern Somalia, the Gerad Dynasty conducted trade with Yemen and Persia and competed with the merchants of the Bari Dynasty. The Gerads and the Bari Sultans built impressive palaces and fortresses and had close relations with many different empires in the Near East. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Garesadihafun.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Garesadihafun.jpg/250px-Garesadihafun.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Garesadihafun.jpg/375px-Garesadihafun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Garesadihafun.jpg/500px-Garesadihafun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="621" data-file-height="350"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Garesadihafun.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>One of the forts of the <a href="/wiki/Majeerteen_Sultanate" title="Majeerteen Sultanate">Majeerteen Sultanate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hafun" title="Hafun">Hafun</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu1936.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mogadishu1936.jpg/250px-Mogadishu1936.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mogadishu1936.jpg/375px-Mogadishu1936.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mogadishu1936.jpg/500px-Mogadishu1936.jpg 2x" data-file-width="565" data-file-height="367"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu1936.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mogadishu_under_Italian_rule" title="Mogadishu under Italian rule">Mogadishu</a>, capital of <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Mogadiscio_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Mogadiscio Cathedral">Catholic Cathedral</a> at the center and the Arch monument in honor of <a href="/wiki/Umberto_I_of_Italy" title="Umberto I of Italy">King Umberto I of Italy</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the late 19th century, after the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a> of 1884, European powers began the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>, which inspired the Dervish leader <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Abdullah_Hassan" title="Mohammed Abdullah Hassan">Mohammed Abdullah Hassan</a> to unify <a href="/wiki/%27Iid" class="mw-redirect" title="'Iid">'Iid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nugaal_Valley" title="Nugaal Valley">Nugaal</a> and begin one of the longest colonial resistance wars in history.<sup id="cite_ref-diiwaanka_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diiwaanka-30">[29]</a></sup> In several of his poems and speeches, Hassan emphasized that the British "have destroyed our religion and made our children their children" and that the Christian Ethiopians in league with the British were bent upon plundering the political and religious freedom of the Somali nation.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> He soon emerged as "a champion of his country's political and religious freedom, defending it against all Christian invaders".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>He soon acquired weapons from the Ottoman Empire, Sudan, other Islamic and Arabian countries, and appointed ministers and advisers to administer different areas or sectors of Somalia. In addition, he gave a clarion call for <a href="/wiki/%27Iid" class="mw-redirect" title="'Iid">'Iid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nugaal_Valley" title="Nugaal Valley">Nugaal</a>'s unity and independence,<sup id="cite_ref-diiwaanka_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diiwaanka-30">[29]</a></sup> in the process of organizing his forces.<sup id="cite_ref-diiwaanka_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diiwaanka-30">[29]</a></sup> Hassan's Dervish movement had an essentially military character, and the Dervish revolt was fashioned on the model of a Salihiya brotherhood. It was characterized by a rigid hierarchy and centralization. Though Hassan threatened to drive the Christians into the sea, he executed the first attack by launching his first major military offensive with 1,500 Dervish equipped with 20 modern rifles on the British soldiers stationed in the region. He repulsed the British in four expeditions and had relations with the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> of the Ottomans and the Germans. In 1920, the Dervish movement collapsed after intensive aerial bombardments by Britain, and Dervish territories were subsequently turned incorporated into the British protectorate. </p>
      <p>The dawn of <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> in the early 1920s heralded a change of strategy for Italy, as the north-eastern sultanates were soon to be forced within the boundaries of <i>La Grande Somalia</i> according to the plan of Fascist Italy. With the arrival of Governor <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Maria_De_Vecchi" title="Cesare Maria De Vecchi">Cesare Maria De Vecchi</a> on 15 December 1923, things began to change for that part of Somaliland known as <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a>. Italy had access to these areas under the successive protection treaties, but not direct rule. </p>
      <p>The Fascist government had direct rule only over the <a href="/wiki/Benadir" class="mw-redirect" title="Benadir">Benadir</a> territory. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy#Fascist_regime_(1922–1943)" title="Kingdom of Italy">Fascist Italy</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>, attacked Abyssinia (Ethiopia) in 1935, with an aim to colonize it. The invasion was condemned by the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, but little was done to stop it or to liberate occupied Ethiopia. On 3 August 1940, Italian troops, including Somali colonial units, crossed from Ethiopia to <a href="/wiki/Italian_conquest_of_British_Somaliland" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian conquest of British Somaliland">invade British Somaliland</a>, and by 14 August, succeeded in taking <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a> from the British. </p>
      <p>A British force, including troops from several African countries, launched the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">campaign in January 1941</a> from Kenya to liberate British Somaliland and Italian-occupied Ethiopia and conquer Italian Somaliland. By February most of Italian Somaliland was captured and, in March, British Somaliland was retaken from the sea. The forces of the British Empire operating in Somaliland comprised the three divisions of South African, West African, and East African troops. They were assisted by Somali forces led by Abdulahi Hassan with Somalis of the <a href="/wiki/Isaaq" title="Isaaq">Isaaq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhulbahante" title="Dhulbahante">Dhulbahante</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warsangali" title="Warsangali">Warsangali</a> clans prominently participating. The number of <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somalis" title="Italian Somalis">Italian Somalis</a> began to decline after World War II, with fewer than 10,000 remaining in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Tripodi1999_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tripodi1999-92">[91]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_.281960.E2.80.931969.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_(1960–1969)">Independence (1960–1969)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Independence (1960–1969)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/State_of_Somaliland" title="State of Somaliland">State of Somaliland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Somalia" title="Greater Somalia">Greater Somalia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Somali_Youth_League" title="Somali Youth League">Somali Youth League</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg/170px-Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg/255px-Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg/340px-Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="464" data-file-height="587"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu_city_centre_-_1960s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An avenue in downtown <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a> in 1963.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following World War II, Britain retained control of both <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British Somaliland</a> and Italian Somaliland as protectorates. In 1945, during the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>, the United Nations granted Italy trusteeship of Italian Somaliland as the <a href="/wiki/Trust_Territory_of_Somaliland" title="Trust Territory of Somaliland">Trust Territory of Somaliland</a>, on the condition first proposed by the Somali Youth League (SYL) and other nascent Somali political organizations, such as Hizbia Digil Mirifle Somali (HDMS) and the Somali National League (SNL)—that Somalia achieve independence within ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-Zolberg_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zolberg-93">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> British Somaliland remained a protectorate of Britain until 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Tripodi1999_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tripodi1999-92">[91]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>To the extent that Italy held the territory by UN mandate, the trusteeship provisions gave the Somalis the opportunity to gain experience in Western political education and self-government. These were advantages that British Somaliland, which was to be incorporated into the new Somali state, did not have. Although in the 1950s British colonial officials attempted, through various administrative development efforts, to make up for past neglect, the protectorate stagnated in political administrative development. The disparity between the two territories in economic development and political experience would later cause serious difficulties integrating the two parts.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Meanwhile, in 1948, under pressure from their World War II allies and to the dismay of the Somalis,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> the British returned the <a href="/wiki/Haud" title="Haud">Haud</a> (an important Somali grazing area that was presumably protected by British treaties with the Somalis in 1884 and 1886) and the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Region" title="Somali Region">Somali Region</a> to Ethiopia, based on a treaty they signed in 1897 in which the British ceded Somali territory to the Ethiopian Emperor <a href="/wiki/Menelek_II_of_Ethiopia" class="mw-redirect" title="Menelek II of Ethiopia">Menelik</a> in exchange for his help against possible advances by the French.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Britain included the conditional provision that the Somali residents would retain their autonomy, but Ethiopia immediately claimed sovereignty over the area. This prompted an unsuccessful bid by Britain in 1956 to buy back the Somali lands it had turned over.<sup id="cite_ref-Zolberg_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zolberg-93">[92]</a></sup> Britain also granted administration of the almost exclusively Somali-inhabited <a href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Frontier District">Northern Frontier District</a> (NFD) to Kenyan nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> This was despite a <a href="/wiki/Plebiscite" class="mw-redirect" title="Plebiscite">plebiscite</a> in which, according to a British colonial commission, almost all of the territory's ethnic Somalis favored joining the newly formed Somali Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland_overseas_territory_referendum,_1958" class="mw-redirect" title="French Somaliland overseas territory referendum, 1958">referendum</a> was held in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> (then known as <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a>) in 1958, on the eve of Somalia's independence in 1960, to decide whether or not to join the Somali Republic or to remain with France. The referendum turned out in favour of a continued association with France, largely due to a combined yes vote by the sizable <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> ethnic group and resident Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-101">[100]</a></sup> There was also widespread vote rigging, with the French expelling thousands of Somalis before the referendum reached the polls.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The majority of those who voted 'no' were Somalis who were strongly in favour of joining a united Somalia, as had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Harbi" title="Mahmoud Harbi">Mahmoud Harbi</a>, Vice President of the Government Council. Harbi was killed in a plane crash two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-101">[100]</a></sup> Djibouti finally gained independence from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> in 1977, and <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon" title="Hassan Gouled Aptidon">Hassan Gouled Aptidon</a>, a Somali who had campaigned for a 'yes' vote in the referendum of 1976, eventually became Djibouti's first president (1977–1999).<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-101">[100]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 1 July 1960, the two territories united to form the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a>, albeit within boundaries drawn up by Italy and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> A government was formed by <a href="/wiki/Abdullahi_Issa" title="Abdullahi Issa">Abdullahi Issa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Haji_Ibrahim_Egal" title="Muhammad Haji Ibrahim Egal">Muhammad Haji Ibrahim Egal</a> with other members of the trusteeship and protectorate governments, with <a href="/wiki/Abdulcadir_Muhammed_Aden" title="Abdulcadir Muhammed Aden">Abdulcadir Muhammed Aden</a> as President of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Somalia">Somali National Assembly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aden_Abdullah_Osman_Daar" class="mw-redirect" title="Aden Abdullah Osman Daar">Aden Abdullah Osman Daar</a> as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of Somalia">President</a> of the Somali Republic, and <a href="/wiki/Abdirashid_Ali_Shermarke" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdirashid Ali Shermarke">Abdirashid Ali Shermarke</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister of Somalia">Prime Minister</a> (later to become president from 1967 to 1969). On 20 July 1961 and through a <a href="/wiki/Somali_constitutional_referendum,_1961" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali constitutional referendum, 1961">popular referendum</a>, was ratified popularly by the people of Somalia under Italian trusteeship, But most of the people from the former Somaliland Protectorate didn't participated the referendum, due to the marginalization graveness made on their rights of power sharing of the unity government. only small number of Somalilanders participated the referendum voted against the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Somalia" title="Constitution of Somalia">new constitution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> which was first drafted in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Illustrated_Library_1967_338._33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Illustrated_Library_1967_338.-33">[32]</a></sup> In 1967, Muhammad Haji Ibrahim Egal became Prime Minister, a position to which he was appointed by Shermarke. Egal would later become the President of the autonomous <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> region in northwestern Somalia. </p>
      <p>On 15 October 1969, while paying a visit to the northern town of <a href="/wiki/Las_Anod" title="Las Anod">Las Anod</a>, Somalia's then President Abdirashid Ali Shermarke was shot dead by one of his own bodyguards. His assassination was quickly followed by a military <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> on 21 October 1969 (the day after his funeral), in which the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Military of Somalia">Somali Army</a> seized power without encountering armed opposition — essentially a bloodless takeover. The putsch was spearheaded by Major General <a href="/wiki/Siad_Barre" title="Siad Barre">Mohamed Siad Barre</a>, who at the time commanded the army.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Somali_Democratic_Republic_.281969.E2.80.931987.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Somali_Democratic_Republic_(1969–1987)">Somali Democratic Republic (1969–1987)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Somali Democratic Republic (1969–1987)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somali Democratic Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/1969_Somali_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1969 Somali coup d'état">1969 Somali coup d'état</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg/220px-Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg/330px-Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg/440px-Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1267" data-file-height="892"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Siad_Barre_-_40866X9X9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Major General <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Siad_Barre" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Siad Barre">Mohamed Siad Barre</a>, Chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Revolutionary_Council_(Somalia)" title="Supreme Revolutionary Council (Somalia)">Supreme Revolutionary Council</a>, meeting with President of Romania <a href="/wiki/Nicolae_Ceau%C5%9Fescu" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicolae Ceauşescu">Nicolae Ceauşescu</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Alongside Barre, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Revolutionary_Council_(Somalia)" title="Supreme Revolutionary Council (Somalia)">Supreme Revolutionary Council</a> (SRC) that assumed power after President Sharmarke's assassination was led by Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Salaad_Gabeyre_Kediye" title="Salaad Gabeyre Kediye">Salaad Gabeyre Kediye</a> and Chief of Police <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jama_Korshel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jama Korshel (page does not exist)">Jama Korshel</a>. Kediye officially held the title "Father of the Revolution", and Barre shortly afterwards became the head of the SRC.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> The SRC subsequently renamed the country the Somali Democratic Republic,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grolierenc_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grolierenc-109">[108]</a></sup> dissolved the parliament and the Supreme Court, and suspended the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Pjdlfw_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pjdlfw-110">[109]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The revolutionary army established large-scale public works programs and successfully implemented an urban and rural <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> campaign, which helped dramatically increase the literacy rate. In addition to a nationalization program of industry and land, the new regime's foreign policy placed an emphasis on Somalia's traditional and religious links with the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>, eventually joining the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> in February, 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> That same year, Barre also served as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity</a> (OAU), the predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> (AU).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In July 1976, Barre's SRC disbanded itself and established in its place the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Revolutionary_Socialist_Party" title="Somali Revolutionary Socialist Party">Somali Revolutionary Socialist Party</a> (SRSP), a one-party government based on <a href="/wiki/Scientific_socialism" title="Scientific socialism">scientific socialism</a> and Islamic tenets. The SRSP was an attempt to reconcile the official state ideology with the official state religion by <a href="/wiki/Revisionism_(Marxism)" title="Revisionism (Marxism)">adapting Marxist precepts</a> to local circumstances. Emphasis was placed on the Muslim principles of social progress, equality and justice, which the government argued formed the core of scientific socialism and its own accent on self-sufficiency, public participation and popular control, as well as direct ownership of the means of production. While the SRSP encouraged private investment on a limited scale, the administration's overall direction was essentially <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pjdlfw_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pjdlfw-110">[109]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In July 1977, the <a href="/wiki/Ogaden_War" title="Ogaden War">Ogaden War</a> broke out after Barre's government used a plea for national unity to justify an <a href="/wiki/War_of_aggression" title="War of aggression">aggressive</a> incorporation of the predominantly Somali-inhabited <a href="/wiki/Ogaden" title="Ogaden">Ogaden</a> region of Ethiopia into a Pan-Somali <a href="/wiki/Greater_Somalia" title="Greater Somalia">Greater Somalia</a>, along with the rich agricultural lands of south-eastern Ethiopia, infrastructure, and strategically important areas as far north as Djibouti.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> In the first week of the conflict, Somali armed forces took southern and central Ogaden and for most of the war, the Somali army scored continuous victories on the Ethiopian army and followed them as far as <a href="/wiki/Sidamo_Province" title="Sidamo Province">Sidamo</a>. By September 1977, Somalia controlled 90% of the Ogaden and captured strategic cities such as <a href="/wiki/Jijiga" title="Jijiga">Jijiga</a> and put heavy pressure on <a href="/wiki/Dire_Dawa" title="Dire Dawa">Dire Dawa</a>, threatening the train route from the latter city to Djibouti. After the siege of Harar, a massive unprecedented Soviet intervention consisting of 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuban</a> forces and several thousand Soviet experts came to the aid of Ethiopia's communist <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> regime. By 1978, the Somali troops were ultimately pushed out of the Ogaden. This shift in support by the Soviet Union motivated the Barre government to seek allies elsewhere. It eventually settled on the Soviets' <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> arch-rival, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, which had been courting the Somali government for some time. All in all, Somalia's initial friendship with the Soviet Union and later partnership with the United States enabled it to build the largest army in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramsbotham_222_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsbotham_222-114">[113]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A new constitution was promulgated in 1979 under which elections for a People's Assembly were held. However, Barre's Somali Revolutionary Socialist Party <a href="/wiki/Politburo" title="Politburo">politburo</a> continued to rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Grolierenc_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grolierenc-109">[108]</a></sup> In October 1980, the SRSP was disbanded, and the Supreme Revolutionary Council was re-established in its place.<sup id="cite_ref-Pjdlfw_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pjdlfw-110">[109]</a></sup> By that time, Barre's government had become increasingly unpopular. Many Somalis had become disillusioned with life under military dictatorship. </p>
      <p>The regime was weakened further in the 1980s as the Cold War drew to a close and Somalia's strategic importance was diminished. The government became increasingly <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Resistance_movement" title="Resistance movement">resistance movements</a>, encouraged by Ethiopia, sprang up across the country, eventually leading to the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a>. Among the militia groups were the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Salvation_Democratic_Front" title="Somali Salvation Democratic Front">Somali Salvation Democratic Front</a> (SSDF), <a href="/wiki/United_Somali_Congress" title="United Somali Congress">United Somali Congress</a> (USC), <a href="/wiki/Somali_National_Movement" title="Somali National Movement">Somali National Movement</a> (SNM) and the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Patriotic_Movement" title="Somali Patriotic Movement">Somali Patriotic Movement</a> (SPM), together with the non-violent political oppositions of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali Democratic Movement">Somali Democratic Movement</a> (SDM), the <a href="/wiki/Factions_in_the_Somali_Civil_War#Somali_Democratic_Alliance_(SDA)" title="Factions in the Somali Civil War">Somali Democratic Alliance</a> (SDA) and the Somali Manifesto Group (SMG). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Somalia_Civil_War">Somalia Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Somalia Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Somalia_(1991%E2%80%932006)" title="History of Somalia (1991–2006)">History of Somalia (1991–2006)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaaq_genocide" title="Isaaq genocide">Isaaq genocide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Somaliland_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Somaliland Declaration of Independence">Somaliland Declaration of Independence</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg/220px-Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg/330px-Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg/440px-Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="684"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Exhumed_skeletal_remains_of_victims_of_the_Isaaq_genocide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Exhumed skeletal remains of victims of the <a href="/wiki/Isaaq_genocide" title="Isaaq genocide">Isaaq genocide</a> found from a mass grave site located in <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Isaaq_genocide_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Isaaq_genocide_map.png/310px-Isaaq_genocide_map.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="436" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Isaaq_genocide_map.png/465px-Isaaq_genocide_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Isaaq_genocide_map.png/620px-Isaaq_genocide_map.png 2x" data-file-width="2637" data-file-height="3711"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Isaaq_genocide_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of the sites related to the Isaaq genocide
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Moral_authority" title="Moral authority">moral authority</a> of Barre's government was gradually eroded, as many Somalis became disillusioned with life under military rule. By the mid-1980s, resistance movements supported by Ethiopia's communist <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> administration had sprung up across the country. Barre responded by ordering punitive measures against those he perceived as locally supporting the guerrillas, especially in the northern regions. The clampdown included bombing of cities, with the northwestern administrative centre of <a href="/wiki/Hargeisa" title="Hargeisa">Hargeisa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Somali_National_Movement" title="Somali National Movement">Somali National Movement</a> (SNM) stronghold, among the targeted areas in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-Locsg_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Locsg-115">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> The bombardment was led by General <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Said_Hersi_Morgan" title="Mohammed Said Hersi Morgan">Mohammed Said Hersi Morgan</a>, Barre's son-in-law.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During 1990, in the capital city of Mogadishu, the residents were prohibited from gathering publicly in groups greater than three or four. Fuel shortages caused long lines of cars at petrol stations. Inflation had driven the price of pasta (ordinary dry Italian noodles, a staple at that time) to five U.S. dollars per kilogram. The price of <a href="/wiki/Khat" title="Khat">khat</a>, imported daily from Kenya, was also five U.S. dollars per standard bunch. Paper currency notes were of such low value that several bundles were needed to pay for simple restaurant meals. </p>
      <p>A thriving black market existed in the centre of the city as banks experienced shortages of local currency for exchange. At night, the city of Mogadishu lay in darkness. Close monitoring of all visiting foreigners was in effect. Harsh <a href="/wiki/Exchange_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange control">exchange control</a> regulations were introduced to prevent export of foreign currency. Although no travel restrictions were placed on foreigners, photographing many locations was banned. During daytime in Mogadishu, the appearance of any government military force was extremely rare. Alleged late-night operations by government authorities, however, included "disappearances" of individuals from their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1991, the Barre administration was ousted by a coalition of clan-based opposition groups, backed by Ethiopia's then-ruling <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Derg</a> regime and <a href="/wiki/Great_Socialist_People%27s_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya">Libya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> Following a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_National_Movement" title="Somali National Movement">Somali National Movement</a> and northern clans' elders, the northern former British portion of the country declared its independence as the <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Republic of Somaliland</a> in May 1991. Although <i lang="la" title="Latin-language text">de facto</i> independent and relatively stable compared to the tumultuous south, it has not been recognized by any foreign government.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mogadishu.jpg/220px-Mogadishu.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mogadishu.jpg/330px-Mogadishu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mogadishu.jpg/440px-Mogadishu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="268"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mogadishu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Prior to the civil war, Mogadishu was known as the "White pearl of the Indian Ocean".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Many of the opposition groups subsequently began competing for influence in the power vacuum that followed the ouster of Barre's regime.
