In the south, armed factions led by USC commanders General <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Farah_Aidid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Farah Aidid">Mohamed Farah Aidid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali_Mahdi_Mohamed" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Mahdi Mohamed">Ali Mahdi Mohamed</a>, in particular, clashed as each sought to exert authority over the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> In 1991, a multi-phased international conference on Somalia was held in neighbouring Djibouti. Aidid boycotted the first meeting in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-Paul_Fricska_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Fricska-124">[123]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Due to the legitimacy bestowed on Muhammad by the Djibouti conference, he was subsequently recognized by the international community as the new President of Somalia. Djibouti, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> were among the countries that officially extended recognition to Muhammad's administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Paul_Fricska_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Fricska-124">[123]</a></sup> He was not able to exert his authority beyond parts of the capital. Power was instead vied with other faction leaders in the southern half of Somalia and with autonomous sub-national entities in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> The Djibouti conference was followed by two abortive agreements for national reconciliation and disarmament, which were signed by 15 political stakeholders: an agreement to hold an Informal Preparatory Meeting on National Reconciliation, and the 1993 Addis Ababa Agreement made at the Conference on National Reconciliation.<sup id="cite_ref-Aspp_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aspp-126">[125]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failed_State_Status_and_International_Intervention">Failed State Status and International Intervention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Failed State Status and International Intervention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In the early 1990s, due to the protracted lack of a permanent central authority, Somalia began to be characterized as a "<a href="/wiki/Failed_state" title="Failed state">failed state</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Menkhaus" title="Kenneth Menkhaus">Ken Menkhaus</a> argues that evidence suggested that the nation had already attained failed state status by the mid-1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Robert_I._Rotberg" title="Robert I. Rotberg">Robert I. Rotberg</a> similarly posits that the state failure had preceded the ouster of the Barre administration.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Hoehne (2009), Branwen (2009) and Verhoeven (2009) also used Somalia during this period as a case study to critique various aspects of the "state failure" discourse.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg/220px-UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg/330px-UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg/440px-UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:UNOSOM_Somalia_tanks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/UNOSOM_I" class="mw-redirect" title="UNOSOM I">UNOSOM</a> in Mogadishu
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_733" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 733">UN Security Council Resolution 733</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_746" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 746">UN Security Council Resolution 746</a> led to the creation of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Operation_in_Somalia_I" title="United Nations Operation in Somalia I">UNOSOM I</a>, the first mission to provide humanitarian relief and help restore order in Somalia after the dissolution of its central government. <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_794" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 794">United Nations Security Council Resolution 794</a> was unanimously passed on 3 December 1992, which approved a coalition of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_peacekeeping" title="United Nations peacekeeping">United Nations peacekeepers</a> led by the United States. Forming the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Task_Force" title="Unified Task Force">Unified Task Force</a> (UNITAF), the alliance was tasked with assuring security until humanitarian efforts aimed at stabilizing the situation were transferred to the UN. Landing in 1993, the UN peacekeeping coalition started the two-year <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Operation_in_Somalia_II" title="United Nations Operation in Somalia II">United Nations Operation in Somalia II</a> (UNOSOM II) primarily in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> UNITAF's original mandate was to use "all necessary means" to guarantee the delivery of humanitarian aid in accordance to <a href="/wiki/Chapter_VII_of_the_United_Nations_Charter" title="Chapter VII of the United Nations Charter">Chapter VII of the United Nations Charter</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> and is regarded as a success.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Aidid saw UNOSOM II as a threat to his power and in June 1993 his militia attacked <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a> troops, attached to UNOSOM II, (see <a href="/wiki/UN_peacekeeping_missions_involving_Pakistan#Somalia_(March_1992_to_February_1996)" class="mw-redirect" title="UN peacekeeping missions involving Pakistan">Somalia (March 1992 to February 1996)</a>) in Mogadishu inflicting over 80 casualties. Fighting escalated until 19 American troops and more than 1,000 civilians and militia were killed in a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mogadishu_(1993)" title="Battle of Mogadishu (1993)">raid in Mogadishu</a> during October 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> The UN withdrew <a href="/wiki/Operation_United_Shield" title="Operation United Shield">Operation United Shield</a> on 3 March 1995, having suffered significant casualties, and with the rule of government still not restored. In August 1996, Aidid was killed in Mogadishu. Former UN Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Boutros_Boutros_Ghali" class="mw-redirect" title="Boutros Boutros Ghali">Boutros Boutros Ghali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmedou_Ould_Abdallah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmedou Ould Abdallah">Ahmedou Ould Abdallah</a>, UN special envoy to Somalia have referred to the killing of civilians during the conflict as a "genocide".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Under the auspices of the UN, AU, Arab League and IGAD, a series of additional national reconciliation conferences were subsequently held as part of the peace process. Among these summits were the 1997 National Salvation Council in Sodere, Ethiopia, the 1997 Cairo Peace Conference / Cairo Declaration, the 2000 Somalia National Peace Conference in Arta, Djibouti under the newly established <a href="/wiki/Transitional_National_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional National Government">Transitional National Government</a>, the 2002 Somali Reconciliation Conference in Eldoret, Kenya, the 2003 National Reconciliation Conference in Nairobi, Kenya when the Transitional Federal Government was established and the Transitional Federal Charter was adopted, the 2004 Nairobi Conference, and the 2007 National Reconciliation Conference in Mogadishu.<sup id="cite_ref-Aspp_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aspp-126">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the outbreak of the civil war, many of Somalia's residents left in search of asylum. According to the <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a>, there were around 975,951 registered <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> from the country in neighboring states as of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Unhcrreg_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unhcrreg-140">[139]</a></sup> Additionally, 1.1 million people were <a href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_person" title="Internally displaced person">internally displaced persons</a> (IDPs).<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> The majority of the IDPs were <a href="/wiki/Bantus_(Somalia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bantus (Somalia)">Bantus</a> and other ethnic minorities originating from the southern regions, including those displaced in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> An estimated 60% of the IDPs were children.<sup id="cite_ref-Idmc_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Idmc-143">[142]</a></sup> Causes of the displacement included armed violence, periodic droughts, and other natural disasters, which, along with diverted aid flows, hindered the IDPs' access to safe shelter and resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Idmc2_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Idmc2-144">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> IDP settlements were concentrated in south-central Somalia (893,000), followed by the northern Puntland (129,000) and Somaliland (84,000) regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Idmc_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Idmc-143">[142]</a></sup> Additionally, there were around 9,356 registered refugees and 11,157 registered <a href="/wiki/Asylum_seeker" title="Asylum seeker">asylum seekers</a> in Somalia.<sup id="cite_ref-Unhcrreg_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unhcrreg-140">[139]</a></sup> Most of these foreign nationals emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> to Berbera after the <a href="/wiki/2015_military_intervention_in_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="2015 military intervention in Yemen">Houthi insurgency</a> in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Grfylibt_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grfylibt-146">[145]</a></sup> However, the majority of emigrants to Somalia consist of Somali expatriates, who have returned to Mogadishu and other urban areas for investment opportunities and to take part in the ongoing post-conflict reconstruction process.<sup id="cite_ref-Meanbd_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meanbd-147">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A consequence of the collapse of governmental authority that accompanied the civil war was the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Piracy in Somalia">piracy</a> in the unpatrolled <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> waters off of the coast of Somalia. The phenomenon partly arose as an attempt by local fishermen to protect their livelihood from illegal fishing by foreigners.<sup id="cite_ref-Tdosp_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tdosp-148">[147]</a></sup> In August 2008, a multinational coalition, <a href="/wiki/Combined_Task_Force_150" title="Combined Task Force 150">Combined Task Force 150</a>, took on the task of combating the piracy by establishing a <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Security_Patrol_Area" title="Maritime Security Patrol Area">Maritime Security Patrol Area</a> (MSPA) within the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> Many ship owners have also hired private armed guards.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> By October 2012, pirate attacks had dropped to a six-year low, with only one ship attacked in the third quarter compared to 36 during the same period in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transitional_institutions">Transitional institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Transitional institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transitional_National_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional National Government">Transitional National Government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Institutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Institutions">Transitional Federal Institutions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Government">Transitional Federal Government</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Parliament">Transitional Federal Parliament</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cqaasim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Cqaasim.jpg/220px-Cqaasim.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Cqaasim.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="225"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cqaasim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Abdiqasim_Salad_Hassan" title="Abdiqasim Salad Hassan">Abdiqasim Salad Hassan</a>, one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_National_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional National Government">Transitional National Government</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Transitional National Government (TNG) was established in April–May 2000 at the Somalia National Peace Conference (SNPC) held in Arta, Djibouti. <a href="/wiki/Abdiqasim_Salad_Hassan" title="Abdiqasim Salad Hassan">Abdiqasim Salad Hassan</a> was selected as the President of the nation's new Transitional National Government (TNG), an interim administration formed to guide Somalia to its third permanent republican government.<sup id="cite_ref-Cfbsom_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cfbsom-152">[151]</a></sup> The TNG's internal problems led to the replacement of the Prime Minister four times in three years, and the administrative body's reported bankruptcy in December 2003. Its mandate ended at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-TNG-PRIME-MINISTER-CONCLUDES-FORMATION-OF-CABINET_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TNG-PRIME-MINISTER-CONCLUDES-FORMATION-OF-CABINET-153">[152]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 10 October 2004, legislators elected <a href="/wiki/Abdullahi_Yusuf_Ahmed" title="Abdullahi Yusuf Ahmed">Abdullahi Yusuf Ahmed</a> as the first President of the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Government">Transitional Federal Government</a> (TFG), the Transitional National Government's successor.<sup id="cite_ref-Spmoarc_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spmoarc-154">[153]</a></sup> the TFG was the second interim administration aiming to restore national institutions to Somalia after the 1991 collapse of the Siad Barre regime and the ensuing civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-fctbk_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fctbk-155">[154]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Transitional Federal Government (TFG) was the internationally recognised government of Somalia until 20 August 2012, when its tenure officially ended.<sup id="cite_ref-Fcacsunesinpshm_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fcacsunesinpshm-39">[38]</a></sup> It was established as one of the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Institutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Institutions">Transitional Federal Institutions</a> (TFIs) of government as defined in the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Charter">Transitional Federal Charter</a> (TFC) adopted in November 2004 by the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional Federal Parliament">Transitional Federal Parliament</a> (TFP). The Transitional Federal Government officially comprised the <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive</a> branch of government, with the TFP serving as the <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislative</a> branch. The government was headed by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Somalia" title="President of Somalia">President of Somalia</a>, to whom the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">cabinet</a> reported through the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister of Somalia">Prime Minister</a>. However, it was also used as a general term to refer to all three branches collectively. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_Courts_Union_and_Ethiopian_intervention">Islamic Courts Union and Ethiopian intervention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Islamic Courts Union and Ethiopian intervention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mogadishu_(2006)" title="Battle of Mogadishu (2006)">Battle of Mogadishu (2006)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Islamic_Courts_Union_(2006)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rise of the Islamic Courts Union (2006)">Rise of the Islamic Courts Union (2006)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Somalia_War_(2006%E2%80%932009)" title="Somalia War (2006–2009)">Somalia War (2006–2009)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Icu_somalia_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Icu_somalia_map.png/330px-Icu_somalia_map.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="428" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Icu_somalia_map.png/495px-Icu_somalia_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Icu_somalia_map.png/660px-Icu_somalia_map.png 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3112"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Icu_somalia_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing the ICU at the peak of its influence.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Islamic Courts Union">Islamic Courts Union</a> (ICU), an <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> organization, assumed control of much of the southern part of the country and promptly imposed <a href="/wiki/Shari%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Shari'a">Shari'a</a>. The Transitional Federal Government sought to reestablish its authority, and, with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_National_Defense_Force" title="Ethiopian National Defense Force">Ethiopian troops</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> peacekeepers and air support by the United States, managed to drive out the rival ICU and solidify its rule.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 8 January 2007, as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ras_Kamboni" title="Battle of Ras Kamboni">Battle of Ras Kamboni</a> raged, TFG President and founder Abdullahi Yusuf Ahmed, a former colonel in the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali Army">Somali Army</a> and decorated war hero, entered Mogadishu with the Ethiopian military support for the first time since being elected to office. The government then relocated to <a href="/wiki/Villa_Somalia" title="Villa Somalia">Villa Somalia</a> in the capital from its interim location in <a href="/wiki/Baidoa" title="Baidoa">Baidoa</a>. This marked the first time since the fall of the Siad Barre regime in 1991 that the federal government controlled most of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosppsdott_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosppsdott-157">[156]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following this defeat, the Islamic Courts Union splintered into several different factions. Some of the more radical elements, including <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(Somalia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Shabaab (Somalia)">Al-Shabaab</a>, regrouped to continue their insurgency against the TFG and oppose the Ethiopian military's presence in Somalia. Throughout 2007 and 2008, Al-Shabaab scored military victories, seizing control of key towns and ports in both central and southern Somalia. At the end of 2008, the group had captured Baidoa but not Mogadishu. By January 2009, Al-Shabaab and other militias had managed to force the Ethiopian troops to retreat, leaving behind an under-equipped African Union peacekeeping force to assist the Transitional Federal Government's troops.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Due to a lack of funding and human resources, an <a href="/wiki/Arms_embargo" title="Arms embargo">arms embargo</a> that made it difficult to re-establish a national security force, and general indifference on the part of the international community, President Yusuf found himself obliged to deploy thousands of troops from Puntland to Mogadishu to sustain the battle against insurgent elements in the southern part of the country. Financial support for this effort was provided by the autonomous region's government. This left little revenue for Puntland's own security forces and civil service employees, leaving the territory vulnerable to piracy and terrorist attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 29 December 2008, Abdullahi Yusuf Ahmed announced before a united parliament in Baidoa his resignation as President of Somalia. In his speech, which was broadcast on national radio, Yusuf expressed regret at failing to end the country's seventeen-year conflict as his government had been mandated to do.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> He also blamed the international community for their failure to support the government, and said that the speaker of parliament would succeed him in office per the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Charter_of_the_Somali_Republic" title="Transitional Federal Charter of the Somali Republic">Charter of the Transitional Federal Government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coalition_government">Coalition government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Coalition government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">Al-Shabaab (militant group)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hizbul_Islam" title="Hizbul Islam">Hizbul Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahlu_Sunna_Waljama%27a" title="Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a">Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_the_Re-liberation_of_Somalia" title="Alliance for the Re-liberation of Somalia">Alliance for the Re-liberation of Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War_(2009%E2%80%93present)" title="Somali Civil War (2009–present)">Somali Civil War (2009–present)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2009_timeline_of_the_War_in_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 timeline of the War in Somalia">2009 timeline of the War in Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg/220px-Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg/330px-Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="303"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mohamed_Abdullah_Omaar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Former Foreign Minister of Somalia <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Abdullahi_Omaar" title="Mohamed Abdullahi Omaar">Mohamed Abdullahi Omaar</a> in a meeting with UNDP Administrator <a href="/wiki/Helen_Clark" title="Helen Clark">Helen Clark</a> and other diplomats at the UN headquarters in New York (May 2009)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Between 31 May and 9 June 2008, representatives of Somalia's federal government and the moderate Alliance for the Re-liberation of Somalia (ARS) group of Islamist rebels participated in peace talks in Djibouti brokered by the former United Nations Special Envoy to Somalia, <a href="/wiki/Ahmedou_Ould-Abdallah" title="Ahmedou Ould-Abdallah">Ahmedou Ould-Abdallah</a>. The conference ended with a signed agreement calling for the withdrawal of Ethiopian troops in exchange for the cessation of armed confrontation. Parliament was subsequently expanded to 550 seats to accommodate ARS members, which then elected <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Sharif_Sheikh_Ahmed" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikh Sharif Sheikh Ahmed">Sheikh Sharif Sheikh Ahmed</a>, the former ARS chairman, to office. President Sharif shortly afterwards appointed <a href="/wiki/Omar_Abdirashid_Ali_Sharmarke" class="mw-redirect" title="Omar Abdirashid Ali Sharmarke">Omar Abdirashid Ali Sharmarke</a>, the son of slain former President <a href="/wiki/Abdirashid_Ali_Sharmarke" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdirashid Ali Sharmarke">Abdirashid Ali Sharmarke</a>, as the nation's new Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With the help of a small team of African Union troops, the coalition government also began a <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War_(2009%E2%80%93present)" title="Somali Civil War (2009–present)">counteroffensive</a> in February 2009 to assume full control of the southern half of the country. To solidify its rule, the TFG formed an alliance with the Islamic Courts Union, other members of the Alliance for the Re-liberation of Somalia, and <a href="/wiki/Ahlu_Sunna_Waljama%27a" title="Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a">Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a</a>, a moderate <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> militia.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> Furthermore, Al-Shabaab and Hizbul Islam, the two main Islamist groups in opposition, began to fight amongst themselves in mid-2009.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As a truce, in March 2009, Somalia's coalition government announced that it would re-implement Shari'a as the nation's official judicial system.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> However, conflict continued in the southern and central parts of the country. Within months, the coalition government had gone from holding about 70% of south-central Somalia's conflict zones, territory that it had inherited from the previous Yusuf administration, to losing control of over 80% of the disputed territory to the Islamist insurgents.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosppsdott_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosppsdott-157">[156]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the coalition government's brief tenure and one year afterwards, due to the protracted lack of a permanent central authority, the <a href="/wiki/Fund_For_Peace" class="mw-redirect" title="Fund For Peace">Fund For Peace</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fragile_States_Index" title="Fragile States Index">Fragile States Index</a> (FSI; formerly known as the Failed States Index) listed Somalia on top for six consecutive years between 2008 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Fsitf_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fsitf-166">[165]</a></sup> In 2009, <a href="/wiki/Transparency_International" title="Transparency International">Transparency International</a> ranked the nation in last place on its annual <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a> (CPI), a metric that purports to show the prevalence of corruption in a country's public sector.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> In mid-2010, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Economics_and_Peace" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute for Economics and Peace">Institute for Economics and Peace</a> also ranked Somalia in the next-to-last position in between war-afflicted Iraq and Afghanistan on its <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Farmaajohd13.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Farmaajohd13.png/220px-Farmaajohd13.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Farmaajohd13.png/330px-Farmaajohd13.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Farmaajohd13.png/440px-Farmaajohd13.png 2x" data-file-width="954" data-file-height="691"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Farmaajohd13.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Abdullahi_Mohamed" title="Mohamed Abdullahi Mohamed">Mohamed Abdullahi Mohamed</a> (Farmajo), President and former Prime Minister of Somalia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 14 October 2010, diplomat <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Abdullahi_Mohamed" title="Mohamed Abdullahi Mohamed">Mohamed Abdullahi Mohamed</a>, also known as Farmajo, was appointed the new Prime Minister of Somalia. The former Premier Omar Abdirashid Ali Sharmarke resigned the month before following a protracted dispute with President Sharif over a proposed draft constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> Per the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Charter_of_the_Somali_Republic" title="Transitional Federal Charter of the Somali Republic">Transitional Federal Charter of the Somali Republic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> Prime Minister Mohamed named a new Cabinet on 12 November 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Nspmusc_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nspmusc-170">[169]</a></sup> which was lauded by the international community.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> As had been expected, the allotted ministerial positions were significantly reduced in numbers from 39 to 18.<sup id="cite_ref-Nspmusc_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nspmusc-170">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spmulc_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spmulc-173">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Additional members of the Independent Constitutional Commission were also appointed to engage Somali constitutional lawyers, religious scholars and experts in Somali culture over the nation's upcoming new constitution, a key part of the government's Transitional Federal Tasks. In addition, high level federal delegations were dispatched to defuse clan-related tensions in several regions. According to the prime minister of Somalia, to improve transparency, Cabinet ministers fully disclosed their assets and signed a code of ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-Swscmos_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swscmos-174">[173]</a></sup> An Anti-Corruption Commission with the power to carry out formal investigations and to review government decisions and protocols was also established to more closely monitor all activities by public officials. Furthermore, unnecessary trips abroad by members of government were prohibited, and all travel by ministers required the Premier's consent.<sup id="cite_ref-Swscmos_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swscmos-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup> A budget outlining 2011's federal expenditures was also put before and approved by members of parliament, with the payment of civil service employees prioritized. In addition, a full audit of government property and vehicles is being put into place.<sup id="cite_ref-Swscmos_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swscmos-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mgamisom_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mgamisom-176">[175]</a></sup> On the war front, the new government and its AMISOM allies also managed to secure control of Mogadishu by August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> According to the African Union and Prime Minister Mohamed, with increasing troop strength the pace of territorial gains was also expected to greatly accelerate.<sup id="cite_ref-Swscmos_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swscmos-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mgamisom_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mgamisom-176">[175]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_government">Federal government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Federal government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg/220px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg/330px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg/440px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="541"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Political map of Somalia.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 19 June 2011, Mohamed Abdullahi Mohamed resigned from his position as Prime Minister of Somalia. Part of the controversial Kampala Accord's conditions, the agreement saw the mandates of the President, the Parliament Speaker and Deputies extended until August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abdiweli_Mohamed_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdiweli Mohamed Ali">Abdiweli Mohamed Ali</a>, Mohamed's former Minister of Planning and International Cooperation, was later named permanent Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-Nytsnnpm_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nytsnnpm-34">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In October 2011, a coordinated operation, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linda_Nchi" title="Operation Linda Nchi">Operation Linda Nchi</a> between the Somali and Kenyan militaries and multinational forces began against the Al-Shabaab group of insurgents in southern Somalia. A joint communiqué was issued indicating that Somali forces were leading operations.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> By September 2012, Somali, Kenyan, and <a href="/wiki/Raskamboni_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Raskamboni movement">Raskamboni</a> forces had managed to capture Al-Shabaab's last major stronghold, the southern port of Kismayo.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> In July 2012, three European Union operations were also launched to engage with Somalia: <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Training_Mission_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union Training Mission Somalia">EUTM Somalia</a>, EU Naval Force Somalia <a href="/wiki/Operation_Atalanta" title="Operation Atalanta">Operation Atalanta</a> off the Horn of Africa, and EUCAP Nestor.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg/220px-Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg/330px-Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg/440px-Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="185"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Parlament_of_Somalia_2012-2016.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Structure of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Parliament_of_Somalia" title="Federal Parliament of Somalia">Federal Parliament of Somalia</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As part of the official "Roadmap for the End of Transition", a political process that provided clear benchmarks leading toward the formation of permanent democratic institutions in Somalia, the Transitional Federal Government's interim mandate ended on 20 August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Aasratdocwau_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aasratdocwau-35">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Parliament_of_Somalia" title="Federal Parliament of Somalia">Federal Parliament of Somalia</a> was concurrently inaugurated.<sup id="cite_ref-Fcacsunesinpshm_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fcacsunesinpshm-39">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Government_of_Somalia" title="Federal Government of Somalia">Federal Government of Somalia</a>, the first permanent central government in the country since the start of the civil war, was later established in August 2012. By 2014, Somalia was no longer at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Fragile_States_Index" title="Fragile States Index">fragile states index</a>, dropping to second place behind <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fsitf_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fsitf-166">[165]</a></sup> UN Special Representative to Somalia <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Kay" title="Nicholas Kay">Nicholas Kay</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> High Representative <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Ashton" title="Catherine Ashton">Catherine Ashton</a> and other international stakeholders and analysts have also begun to describe Somalia as a "<a href="/wiki/Fragile_state" title="Fragile state">fragile state</a>" that is making some progress towards stability.<sup id="cite_ref-Balthasar_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balthasar-183">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FSI2013_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FSI2013-184">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ateua_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ateua-185">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gsyod_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gsyod-186">[185]</a></sup> In August 2014, the Somali government-led <a href="/wiki/Operation_Indian_Ocean" title="Operation Indian Ocean">Operation Indian Ocean</a> was launched against insurgent-held pockets in the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup> The war continued in 2017. </p>
      <p>In October 2017, more than 500 people were killed by <a href="/wiki/14_October_2017_Mogadishu_bombings" title="14 October 2017 Mogadishu bombings">twin bomb explosions</a> in Somalia's capital city <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Somalia" title="Geography of Somalia">Geography of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions_and_districts">Regions and districts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Regions and districts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Somalia" title="Administrative divisions of Somalia">Administrative divisions of Somalia</a> and <a href="/wiki/States_and_regions_of_Somalia" title="States and regions of Somalia">States and regions of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia is officially divided into thirteen <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Somalia" title="Administrative divisions of Somalia">regions</a> and five claimed regions (<i>gobollada</i>, singular <i>gobol</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> which in turn are subdivided into districts. The regions are: </p>

        </div>
       </div>
      </div>

      <p>Northern Somalia is now <i>de facto</i> divided up among the <a href="/wiki/States_and_regions_of_Somalia" title="States and regions of Somalia">autonomous regions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Puntland" title="Puntland">Puntland</a> (which considers itself an <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous state">autonomous state</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> (a self-declared but <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_limited_recognition" title="List of states with limited recognition">unrecognized sovereign state</a>). In central Somalia, <a href="/wiki/Galmudug" title="Galmudug">Galmudug</a> is another regional entity that emerged just south of Puntland. <a href="/wiki/Jubaland" title="Jubaland">Jubaland</a> in the far south is a fourth autonomous region within the federation.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2014, a new <a href="/wiki/South_West_State_of_Somalia" title="South West State of Somalia">Southwestern Somalia</a> was likewise established.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> In April 2015, a formation conference was also launched for a new <a href="/wiki/Central_Regions_State" title="Central Regions State">Central Regions State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Federal Parliament is tasked with selecting the ultimate number and boundaries of the autonomous regional states (officially <i>Federal Member States</i>) within the Federal Republic of Somalia.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Almadow_Overview.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Almadow_Overview.JPG/220px-Almadow_Overview.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Almadow_Overview.JPG/330px-Almadow_Overview.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Almadow_Overview.JPG/440px-Almadow_Overview.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Almadow_Overview.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Cal_Madow" title="Cal Madow">Cal Madow</a> mountain range in northern Somalia features the nation's highest peak, <a href="/wiki/Shimbiris" class="mw-redirect" title="Shimbiris">Shimbiris</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia is bordered by Kenya to the southwest, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Guardafui_Channel" title="Guardafui Channel">Guardafui Channel</a> and Indian Ocean to the east, and Ethiopia to the west. The country claims a border with <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> through the disputed territory of <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> to the northwest. It lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/2nd_parallel_south" title="2nd parallel south">2°S</a> and <a href="/wiki/12th_parallel_north" title="12th parallel north">12°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/41st_meridian_east" title="41st meridian east">41°</a> and <a href="/wiki/52nd_meridian_east" title="52nd meridian east">52°E</a>. Strategically located at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Bab_el_Mandeb" class="mw-redirect" title="Bab el Mandeb">Bab el Mandeb</a> gateway to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, the country occupies the tip of a region that, due to its resemblance on the map to a <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>' horn, is commonly referred to as the Horn of Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waters">Waters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Waters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islands_of_Somalia" title="Islands of Somalia">Islands of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia has the longest coastline on the mainland of Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> with a seaboard that stretches 3,333 kilometres (2,071&nbsp;mi). Its terrain consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Highland" title="Highland">highlands</a>. The nation has a total area of 637,657 square kilometres (246,201&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) of which constitutes land, with 10,320 square kilometres (3,980&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) of water. Somalia's land boundaries extend to about 2,340 kilometres (1,450&nbsp;mi); 58 kilometres (36&nbsp;mi) of that is shared with Djibouti, 682 kilometres (424&nbsp;mi) with Kenya, and 1,626 kilometres (1,010&nbsp;mi) with Ethiopia. Its maritime claims include <a href="/wiki/Territorial_waters" title="Territorial waters">territorial waters</a> of 200 nautical miles (370&nbsp;km; 230&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Somalia has several islands and archipelagos on its coast, including the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_Islands" title="Bajuni Islands">Bajuni Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saad_ad-Din_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Saad ad-Din Islands">Saad ad-Din Archipelago</a>: see <a href="/wiki/Islands_of_Somalia" title="Islands of Somalia">islands of Somalia</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg/220px-Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg/330px-Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg/440px-Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Juba_river_downstream_Jamaame.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Jubba_River" title="Jubba River">Jubba River</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Somalia contains seven terrestrial ecoregions: <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_montane_forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian montane forests">Ethiopian montane forests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Zanzibar-Inhambane_coastal_forest_mosaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Zanzibar-Inhambane coastal forest mosaic">Northern Zanzibar-Inhambane coastal forest mosaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_Acacia-Commiphora_bushlands_and_thickets" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali Acacia-Commiphora bushlands and thickets">Somali Acacia-Commiphora bushlands and thickets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_xeric_grasslands_and_shrublands" title="Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands">Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hobyo_grasslands_and_shrublands" title="Hobyo grasslands and shrublands">Hobyo grasslands and shrublands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_montane_xeric_woodlands" title="Somali montane xeric woodlands">Somali montane xeric woodlands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_African_mangroves" title="East African mangroves">East African mangroves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-195">[194]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the north, a scrub-covered, semi-desert plain referred as the <i><a href="/wiki/Guban" title="Guban">Guban</a></i> lies parallel to the Gulf of Aden <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral</a>. With a width of twelve kilometres in the west to as little as two kilometres in the east, the plain is bisected by watercourses that are essentially beds of dry sand except during the rainy seasons. When the rains arrive, the Guban's low bushes and grass clumps transform into lush vegetation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> This coastal strip is part of the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_xeric_grasslands_and_shrublands" title="Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands">Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Cal_Madow" title="Cal Madow">Cal Madow</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a> in the northeastern part of the country. Extending from several kilometres west of the city of <a href="/wiki/Bosaso" title="Bosaso">Bosaso</a> to the northwest of <a href="/wiki/Erigavo" title="Erigavo">Erigavo</a>, it features Somalia's highest <a href="/wiki/Summit_(topography)" class="mw-redirect" title="Summit (topography)">peak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shimbiris" class="mw-redirect" title="Shimbiris">Shimbiris</a>, which sits at an elevation of about 2,416 metres (7,927&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> The rugged east–west ranges of the Karkaar Mountains also lie to the interior of the Gulf of Aden littoral.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> In the central regions, the country's northern mountain ranges give way to shallow plateaus and typically dry watercourses that are referred to locally as the <i>Ogo</i>. The Ogo's western plateau, in turn, gradually merges into the <a href="/wiki/Haud" title="Haud">Haud</a>, an important grazing area for livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Somalia has only two permanent rivers, the <a href="/wiki/Jubba_River" title="Jubba River">Jubba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shebelle_River" title="Shebelle River">Shabele</a>, both of which begin in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>. These rivers mainly flow southwards, with the Jubba River entering the Indian Ocean at <a href="/wiki/Kismayo" title="Kismayo">Kismayo</a>. The Shabele River at one time apparently used to enter the sea near <a href="/wiki/Merca" title="Merca">Merca</a>, but now reaches a point just southwest of Mogadishu. After that, it consists of swamps and dry reaches before finally disappearing in the desert terrain east of <a href="/wiki/Jilib" title="Jilib">Jilib</a>, near the Jubba River.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Somcoralreef.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Somcoralreef.jpg/330px-Somcoralreef.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="487" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Somcoralreef.jpg/495px-Somcoralreef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Somcoralreef.jpg/660px-Somcoralreef.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1320" data-file-height="1949"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Somcoralreef.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Somalia's <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>, ecological parks and protected areas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia is a <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-arid</a> country with about 1.64% <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> The first local environmental organizations were Ecoterra Somalia and the Somali Ecological Society, both of which helped promote awareness about ecological concerns and mobilized environmental programs in all governmental sectors as well as in civil society. From 1971 onward, a massive tree-planting campaign on a nationwide scale was introduced by the Siad Barre government to halt the advance of thousands of acres of wind-driven <a href="/wiki/Sand_dune" class="mw-redirect" title="Sand dune">sand dunes</a> that threatened to engulf towns, roads and farm land.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> By 1988, 265 hectares of a projected 336 hectares had been treated, with 39 range reserve sites and 36 forestry plantation sites established.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> In 1986, the Wildlife Rescue, Research and Monitoring Centre was established by Ecoterra International, with the goal of sensitizing the public to ecological issues. This educational effort led in 1989 to the so-called "Somalia proposal" and a decision by the Somali government to adhere to the <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora</a> (CITES), which established for the first time a worldwide ban on the trade of elephant <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_(8071381265).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg/220px-Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg/330px-Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg/440px-Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_%288071381265%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3322" data-file-height="2099"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_views_of_Kismayo_06_(8071381265).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The coast south of Mogadishu
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Later, <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Jibrell" title="Fatima Jibrell">Fatima Jibrell</a>, a prominent Somali environmental activist, mounted a successful campaign to salvage old-growth forests of <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">acacia</a> trees in the northeastern part of Somalia.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilbert_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert-197">[196]</a></sup> These trees, which can live for 500 years, were being cut down to make charcoal which was highly in demand in the Arabian Peninsula, where the region's <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> tribes believe the acacia to be sacred.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilbert_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert-197">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IWD_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IWD-199">[198]</a></sup> However, while being a relatively inexpensive fuel that meets a user's needs, the production of charcoal often leads to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IWD_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IWD-199">[198]</a></sup> As a way of addressing this problem, Jibrell and the Horn of Africa Relief and Development Organization (Horn Relief; now <a href="/wiki/Adeso" title="Adeso">Adeso</a>), an organization of which she was the founder and Executive Director, trained a group of teens to educate the public on the permanent damage that producing charcoal can create. In 1999, Horn Relief coordinated a peace march in the northeastern Puntland region of Somalia to put an end to the so-called "charcoal wars". As a result of Jibrell's lobbying and education efforts, the Puntland government in 2000 prohibited the exportation of charcoal. The government has also since enforced the ban, which has reportedly led to an 80% drop in exports of the product.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldman_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-200">[199]</a></sup> Jibrell was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Environmental_Prize" title="Goldman Environmental Prize">Goldman Environmental Prize</a> in 2002 for her efforts against environmental degradation and desertification.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldman_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-200">[199]</a></sup> In 2008, she also won the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>/<a href="/wiki/Buffett_Foundation" title="Buffett Foundation">Buffett Foundation</a> Award for Leadership in Conservation.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the massive <a href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" class="mw-redirect" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">tsunami of December 2004</a>, there have also emerged allegations that after the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a> in the late 1980s, Somalia's long, remote shoreline was used as a dump site for the disposal of toxic waste. The huge waves that battered northern Somalia after the tsunami are believed to have stirred up tons of nuclear and toxic waste that might have been dumped illegally in the country by foreign firms.<sup id="cite_ref-Toxic_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toxic-202">[201]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Green_Party" title="European Green Party">European Green Party</a> followed up these revelations by presenting before the press and the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a> copies of contracts signed by two European companies — the Italian Swiss firm, Achair Partners, and an Italian <a href="/wiki/Waste_broker" title="Waste broker">waste broker</a>, Progresso — and representatives of the then President of Somalia, the faction leader Ali Mahdi Mohamed, to accept 10 million tonnes of toxic waste in exchange for $80 million (then about £60 million).<sup id="cite_ref-Toxic_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toxic-202">[201]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to reports by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP), the waste has resulted in far higher than normal cases of respiratory infections, mouth ulcers and bleeding, abdominal haemorrhages and unusual skin infections among many inhabitants of the areas around the northeastern towns of <a href="/wiki/Hobyo" title="Hobyo">Hobyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benadir" class="mw-redirect" title="Benadir">Benadir</a> on the Indian Ocean coast — diseases consistent with radiation sickness. UNEP adds that the situation along the Somali coastline poses a very serious environmental hazard not only in Somalia, but also in the eastern Africa sub-region.<sup id="cite_ref-Toxic_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toxic-202">[201]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/290px-Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/435px-Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/580px-Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Somalia map of Köppen climate classification.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Due to Somalia's proximity to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, there is not much seasonal variation in its climate. Hot conditions prevail year-round along with periodic <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds and irregular rainfall. Mean daily maximum temperatures range from 30 to 40&nbsp;°C (86 to 104&nbsp;°F), except at higher elevations along the eastern seaboard, where the effects of a cold offshore current can be felt. In Mogadishu, for instance, average afternoon highs range from 28 to 32&nbsp;°C (82 to 90&nbsp;°F) in April. Some of the highest mean annual temperatures in the world have been recorded in the country; <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a> on the northwestern coast has an afternoon high that averages more than 38&nbsp;°C (100&nbsp;°F) from June through September. Nationally, mean daily minimums usually vary from about 15 to 30&nbsp;°C (59 to 86&nbsp;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> The greatest range in climate occurs in northern Somalia, where temperatures sometimes surpass 45&nbsp;°C (113&nbsp;°F) in July on the littoral plains and drop below the freezing point during December in the highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-ClimateSom_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClimateSom-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> In this region, relative humidity ranges from about 40% in the mid-afternoon to 85% at night, changing somewhat according to the season.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> Unlike the climates of most other countries at this latitude, conditions in Somalia range from arid in the northeastern and central regions to <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semiarid</a> in the northwest and south. In the northeast, annual rainfall is less than 100&nbsp;mm (4&nbsp;in); in the central plateaus, it is about 200 to 300&nbsp;mm (8 to 12&nbsp;in). The northwestern and southwestern parts of the nation, however, receive considerably more rain, with an average of 510 to 610&nbsp;mm (20 to 24&nbsp;in) falling per year. Although the coastal regions are hot and humid throughout the year, the hinterland is typically dry and hot.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are four main seasons around which pastoral and agricultural life revolve, and these are dictated by shifts in the wind patterns. From December to March is the <i>Jilal</i>, the harshest dry season of the year. The main rainy season, referred to as the <i>Gu</i>, lasts from April to June. This period is characterized by the southwest monsoons, which rejuvenate the pasture land, especially the central plateau, and briefly transform the desert into lush vegetation. From July to September is the second dry season, the <i>Xagaa</i> (pronounced "Hagaa"). The <i>Dayr</i>, which is the shortest rainy season, lasts from October to December.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> The <i>tangambili</i> periods that intervene between the two monsoons (October–November and March–May) are hot and humid.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadden_193-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadden-193">[192]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Somalia" title="Wildlife of Somalia">Wildlife of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sambalaxx.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sambalaxx.jpg/220px-Sambalaxx.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sambalaxx.jpg/330px-Sambalaxx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sambalaxx.jpg/440px-Sambalaxx.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="500"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sambalaxx.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A camel in the northern mountains.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia contains a variety of mammals due to its geographical and climatic diversity. Wildlife still occurring includes <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">reticulated giraffe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baboon" title="Baboon">baboon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serval" title="Serval">serval</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">elephant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bushpig" title="Bushpig">bushpig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibex" title="Ibex">ibex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kudu" title="Kudu">kudu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dik-dik" title="Dik-dik">dik-dik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oribi" title="Oribi">oribi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_wild_ass" title="Somali wild ass">Somali wild ass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reedbuck" title="Reedbuck">reedbuck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A9vy%27s_zebra" title="Grévy's zebra">Grévy's zebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elephant_shrew" title="Elephant shrew">elephant shrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_hyrax" title="Rock hyrax">rock hyrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_mole" title="Golden mole">golden mole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a>. It also has a large population of the <a href="/wiki/Dromedary_camel" class="mw-redirect" title="Dromedary camel">dromedary camel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mukasa81_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mukasa81-203">[202]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Somalia is home to around 727 species of birds. Of these, eight are endemic, one has been introduced by humans, and one is rare or accidental. Fourteen species are globally threatened. Birds species found exclusively in the country include the <i><a href="/wiki/Columba_oliviae" class="mw-redirect" title="Columba oliviae">Somali Pigeon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alaemon_hamertoni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alaemon hamertoni">Alaemon hamertoni</a></i> (Alaudidae), Lesser Hoopoe-Lark, <i><a href="/wiki/Heteromirafra_archeri" class="mw-redirect" title="Heteromirafra archeri">Heteromirafra archeri</a></i> (Alaudidae), Archer's Lark, <i><a href="/wiki/Mirafra_ashi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mirafra ashi">Mirafra ashi</a></i>, Ash's Bushlark, <i><a href="/wiki/Mirafra_somalica" class="mw-redirect" title="Mirafra somalica">Mirafra somalica</a></i> (Alaudidae), Somali Bushlark, <i><a href="/wiki/Spizocorys_obbiensis" class="mw-redirect" title="Spizocorys obbiensis">Spizocorys obbiensis</a></i> (Alaudidae), Obbia Lark, <i><a href="/wiki/Carduelis_johannis" class="mw-redirect" title="Carduelis johannis">Carduelis johannis</a></i> (Fringillidae), and Warsangli Linnet.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Somalia's territorial waters are prime fishing grounds for highly migratory marine species, such as tuna. A narrow but productive continental shelf contains several <a href="/wiki/Demersal" class="mw-redirect" title="Demersal">demersal</a> fish and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustacean</a> species.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> Fish species found exclusively in the nation include <i>Cirrhitichthys randalli</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cirrhitidae" title="Cirrhitidae">Cirrhitidae</a>), <i>Symphurus fuscus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cynoglossidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Cynoglossidae">Cynoglossidae</a>), <i>Parapercis simulata</i> OC (<a href="/wiki/Pinguipedidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinguipedidae">Pinguipedidae</a>), <i>Cociella somaliensis</i> OC (<a href="/wiki/Platycephalidae" title="Platycephalidae">Platycephalidae</a>), and <i>Pseudochromis melanotus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Pseudochromidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudochromidae">Pseudochromidae</a>). </p>
      <p>There are roughly 235 species of reptiles. Of these, almost half live in the northern areas. Reptiles endemic to Somalia include the Hughes' <a href="/wiki/Saw-scaled_viper" class="mw-redirect" title="Saw-scaled viper">saw-scaled viper</a>, the Southern Somali garter snake, a racer (<i>Platyceps messanai</i>), a diadem snake (<i>Spalerosophis josephscorteccii</i>), the Somali <a href="/wiki/Sand_boa" class="mw-redirect" title="Sand boa">sand boa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Angled_worm_lizard" title="Angled worm lizard">angled worm lizard</a>, a spiny-tailed lizard (<i>Uromastyx macfadyeni</i>), Lanza's agama, a gecko (<i>Hemidactylus granchii</i>), the Somali semaphore <a href="/wiki/Gecko" title="Gecko">gecko</a>, and a sand lizard (<a href="/wiki/Mesalina" title="Mesalina">Mesalina</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eremias" title="Eremias">Eremias</a>). A colubrid snake (<i>Aprosdoketophis andreonei</i>) and Haacke-Greer's skink (<i>Haackgreerius miopus</i>) are endemic species.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_government">Politics and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somalia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Politics and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Somalia" title="Politics of Somalia">Politics of Somalia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jawari.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jawari.png/170px-Jawari.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jawari.png/255px-Jawari.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jawari.png/340px-Jawari.png 2x" data-file-width="505" data-file-height="574"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jawari.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Osman_Jawari" title="Mohamed Osman Jawari">Mohamed Osman Jawari</a>, Former Speaker of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Parliament_of_Somalia" title="Federal Parliament of Somalia">Federal Parliament</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalia is a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Somalia" title="President of Somalia">President of Somalia</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Armed_Forces" title="Somali Armed Forces">Somali Armed Forces</a> and selects a <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Somalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister of Somalia">Prime Minister</a> to act as <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Parliament_of_Somalia" title="Federal Parliament of Somalia">Federal Parliament of Somalia</a> is the national parliament of Somalia. The bicameral National Legislature consists of the House of the People (lower house) and the Senate (upper house), whose members are elected to serve four-year terms. The parliament elects the President, Speaker of Parliament and Deputy Speakers. It also has the authority to pass and veto laws.<sup id="cite_ref-Ajssihnp_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ajssihnp-208">[207]</a></sup> </p>
